RATING: 8
An economic, optimize your own move game that has two interesting bidding phases to it.

This a very thematic game (for Knizia) where you are acquiring (bidding) and developing your own provinces (through the use of money, which can be very tight), and then making a sacrifice to the gods (blind-bidding), and getting the harvest from your provinces (income). 

The game is essentially played in two parts: the old kingdom, and the new kingdom, with all of the development from the old kingdom (excluding farmers) being carried over to the new kingdom, BUT you lose ownership of your old provinces, and may or may not be able to re-aquire them in the new kingdom.  A scoring phase happens afer the completion of each kingdom.  

There are many ways to score points, as per most Knizia games: certain power card requirements met, completed pyramids, sets of pyramids (encourages you to develop each of your provinces), majorities of pyramids (encourages you to not develop each of your provinces), temples (if you're fortunate enough to have acquired them), and most money (only for the new kingdom).

There is alot of strategy to this game, as you have to plan out which provinces you want to obtain, how much you're willing/able to spend to get them (there are some very neat nuances with the bidding system here too, such as not being able to re-bid on a province you've been overbid on, and the perfectly determined increases in cost for each bid), how to manage your development of your provinces (again, the costs are perfect, in that it gets VERY expensive to buy too much at once)- should you go for farmers=money, stones/pyramids=points, should you try your luck at the power cards...lots of tough economic decisions.  Plus the sacrifice can make or break you financially, and is definitely a tense but fun event.

This game works for 3-5, although I prefer 4-5, as it can get very competitive.  This is one of my wife's favourites for reasons unknown to me, and I thoroughly enjoy it as well.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
One of the last great Knizia games. Each turn consists of a round of uniquely styled bidding--often called an "Amun-Re auction" because it originated here but you can see it in games like Homesteaders and Cyclades--followed by the players making an offering to Amun-Re. 

The first auction gets most of the press, but the offering itself is a kind of communal bid. Amun-Re sends more rain the more he is offered. But rain benefits some more than others, with some even being hurt by it. And, of course, no one wants to spend more money then they have to. It's a decision that is simultaneously thematic, interactive, complex, non-fiddly, and quick. This is why Knizia's a genius, folks.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.03
Abstract:
A complex auction game by Reiner Knizia. Players bid for provinces in Egypt, harvest for income, and build pyramids for victory points. The game is split into two eras: after the 3rd round, players remove all farmers and control markers from the board but leave all built pyramids and stones.

Round overview:
a) Reveal province cards
b) Bid on provinces
c) Buy power cards (for abilities), farmers (for harvest), and stones (for pyramids)
d) Sacrifice to Amun-Re for boni and to define harvest outcome and temple value
e) Harvest and receive income 
f) Scoring after the 3rd and 6th round

Pros:
+ Theme (seems abstract but I find it quite thematic)
+ Nice artwork for both: the old and new edition (Egypt theme, pyramids, board and cards have a clean look)
+ Bidding system: you have to bid for another province if you have been outbid (instead of raising bids back and forth, players have to make more realistic bids)
+ High player interaction: bidding for provinces, all players compete for sacrificing most money to get additional boni and they define the income of the harvest / points for temples
+ Dilemma: players work together but also have to keep their personal goals in mind
+ Crunchy gameplay where each decision matters
+ Ownership and value of provinces change after the first era which makes bidding even more interesting
+ Provinces provide different boni
+ Many paths to victory
+ Power cards give players additional options (cards are random but all of them can be useful)
+ Increasing cost (triangular numbers) of farmers, stone, or cards results into hard decision-making: what to buy and how much?
+ Each game has other combinations of provinces. Each province has different boni

Neutrals:
# Almost completely strategic except for randomly drawn power cards and randomly revealed provinces
# All money and power cards of players is hidden information (players may memorize how much money other players possess)
# No direct take-that cards

Cons:
- Best with higher player counts (especially 5 players)
- Not hard to learn but difficult to get into due to complex interaction (should be played frequently with the same people)
- New players will have trouble identifying the real value of something they are bidding on and what is scored at the end of an era
- No catch-up mechanism (can be punishing)
- Players sometimes draw very lucky / unlucky power cards (useless cards can be discarded for 1 gold)

Thoughts:
I like the theme and look of the game board with all its pyramids. What love is the intertwined interaction between players. It is not for everyone though because of its complexity. You need to play Amun-Re at least a few times to really get into the game.

Similar games:
* Ra (simpler, more elegant auction game by Reiner Knizia)
* Cyclades (dudes-on-the-map area control game that uses the same bidding mechanism)
* Goa

Player: 3-5 (best: 4-5) | Time: 90m

RATING: 10
This is a really exciting and very hard game. Valuations of the regions in the six auction phases is generally very hard. The game theory in predicting the best response of other players in the sacrifice can often be really tricky. Then there is the issue of resource allocation across pyramid sets, highest on a side, most money and power cards. Really really tricky and hard valuation decisions to be made at each step. There is some luck element but not so much that you feel helpless. It is really satisfying when you make a right call in this game. Absolutely spectacular game by Knizia. 

I would be interested in trying a variant where the power cards are available to everyone at all times, meaning that anybody who owns real estate on one side of the nile gets three points, whether you own a card or not.
 
Also, we could start with one bid block and same area bid card which we could choose to judicially use once at some point over the six auction phases.

RATING: 6
4-5(5)

I liked it ... I think I might like some of Knizia's other designs better. Taj Mahal just feels "cleaner" in that the regions are just sort of dynamically determined. The region order is random, the elephant tile is random, the advisors are fixed. In Amun Re each region has been balanced in just a particular way, which feels just a bit artificial, though it does make the regions variously interesting in different ways and to different people.

Which is, I guess, the way it has to be.

The two ages deal is interesting in how it can (or at least should) swing the auction dynamics a bit (making building in Age 1 a bit less valuable and prebuilt regions in Age 2 more valuable).

It felt like some players in particular were overloaded with power cards that they couldn't use. Yes, you can discard them for gold, but this sort of this felt very oddly ... superfluous for a Knizia game.

RATING: 8
A game of money management really.  The auctions for land provide some tough judgments of worth, with multiple factors in play (potential income, bonus cards/blocks, temples, scoring bonuses, pyramids). Then there's how you pay more per card, farmer and block the more you buy of them.  And what income will you get from those farmers anyway?  In the offering you are reckoning the value of one, two or three resources to you and your opponents, and how this will effect income for farmers and camels.  Run short of money at a critical moment and watch your dreams of victory turn to dust. There's as much going on in this game as in Taj Mahal, yet I find Amun-Re a more accessible design. The idea of the two epochs, with everything except the pyramids swept away in-between, is a clever and thematic one.  Physical production and artwork are up to Hans im Gluck's usual standards.

RATING: 7
A good game, well-designed with a number of interesting mechanics that are worth thinking about.  Do these mechanics work?  I would say yes, they work.  Are they fun to play with?  I would say fairly so.  The auction mechanic is clever (Homesteaders uses the same one), but with my four player game, that auction mechanic felt a bit flat and non-competitive.  Too often, we would settle for essentially dividing up the four properties with little or no competition. Maybe with five players, or greater knowledge of the relative property values, it would improve.

I like the two building phases aspect of the game, wherein you clean off the board after the first phase, but leave the pyramids, reminds me a bit of the two building phases of Brass. I think the farmer and camel income systems work well enough, but it just didn't feel that compelling to think about.  And the sacrifice phase has its moments, but curiously, felt a bit underwhelming.

This is a game that presumably gets better as players better learn the relative values of each scoring system in the game.  It then, presumably, becomes a study of a scoring system, and how to "game it" best.  That to me, feels a little dry.  The game Ra is similar in this respect, but at least with Ra, the auctions feel a bit more directly confrontational and frothy.

All in all, a game that deserves to be played at least once, and clearly respected for its interesting mechanics, but curiously, this is not a game that I am immediately eager to play again after the first play, except, perhaps, with five players to see if it "heats up" a bit.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Knizia is such a prolific designer that it is hard to describe a game as "one of Knizia's finest", yet that is often how I feel about Amun-Re. 

In this game the key mechanism that is repeated throughout, in multiple places and ways is the payment of money in forced steps: 1, 3, 6, 10, 15, etc. Each raised bid costs exponentially more than what the current bidder has to pay that it makes it a tough propisition to properly evaluate the value of a property.

Then you move to buying resources. There are three kinds: Cards, Farmers, and Stones. Each of them cost more and more the more of them you buy. Again, it makes evaluating your purchases difficult, as you want to make sure your investments payoff in money or victory points. Deliciously tense money management.

Finally, there is the blind-bidding for extra resources, known as the offering. You have a choice here: Steal money and lose a resource, offer just one gold and get one resource, or offer more and try for 2 or 3 resources. It gets very difficult to determine the optimal offering as you have to try to guess what each opponent will do and how that will affect what you should offer. Finally, the mechanism of rewarding the farmers (and on scoring rounds, the temples) based on the value of the total offering, can really throw those calculations out of whack.

There is so much going on that the game could have become a brain-burner, but there never seems to be a problem with that in the games I am in. All-in-all, the intricately interlocking mechanisms and the wonderful tension in spending your money carefully makes this one of my favorite games.

RATING: 8
The consensus is that this game scales the best at five. When you do achieve that number, the game play is beautiful and engrossing.

 The auctions are among the most tense in gaming and the two generation scoring coupled with the ability of players to influence each others income during the turn end "sacrifice" leaves a lot of food for thought for players. Toss in the power cards and the chess game just got better.

This is one of Reiner Knizia's best work and highly recommended if you can get five at the table.

It's not the easiest game to just pick up and play and the theme is as pasted on as they come.  But, as is typical with Knizia, the bidding mechanics and unbalanced auctions are excellent.  Our games seem to drag on a bit and the power cards seem to have too big an impact on points at the end of the game for what is, otherwise, a calculating Euro.

My fourth play was with four other gamers and I've bumped up my rating from 7.5 to 8.5.  Clearly, this is how the game was meant to be played.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a two-round resource-management game in which players bid for areas on the board (provinces), and improve upon those areas to generate money (using farmers), to get cards (special abilities and extra scoring opportunities), and to build pyramids, which are the primary source for victory points.

A detail that sets this game apart is that the game is played in two phases, the Old Kingdom and the New Kingdom, and that pyramids built in the Old Kingdom last into the new, which can drastically alter a province's value.

I highly reccomend this game to players who enjoy auctions and don't mind some moderate luck.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Great auction game with some pretty unique elements.  The auction mechanic is the same as in Vegas Showdown, although this game may have been first- basically if you are outbid you have to bid on something else.  It's a very good mechanism that makes the auctions fun and quick.  The game has two big phases with a reset in the middle when you move from the old to the new kingdom with only pyramids and stones staying on the board.  I love Ra and this has the same theme and same designer and both auction games.  But they're very different games so a comparison isn't as useful as you would thing.  Starts a little slow but builds a great amount of tension throughout- especially in the final rounds.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Along with Ra, one of my favorite Knizia games. Great with 5 players. It shines in two categories especially: the initial bidding for provinces in which you can attempt to trump other players, and the sacrifice to Amun-Re phase. The second half of the game (New Kingdom) offers new opportunities for scoring which benefits players that might not have done well in the first half (Old Kingdom). Spending gold during the sacrifice phase for benefits is a real challenge, as one can miss opportunities as well as overspend. Great game with each phase requiring careful decision-making. UPDATE: This was my 1,000th logged play - at BGG.CON - and a narrow 5 player victory over the likes of Marshall and Jared with a farmer strategy!

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Starting to tire of the phase 1- auction / phase 2- individual actions without any board interaction. There's just something about this game that really repels me. 

First, I hate drawing (as opposed to drafting) 'special ability cards' in games. It always feels like a design failure to me. It's a cheap way to create variety, flavor or false tension/complexity. You better hope you draw the power cards that fit in with what you are doing. 

It's very mundane and repetitive. The way the prices escalate, it seems every turn every player is buying 2 or 3 power cards, 2 or 3 farmers, 2 or 3 bricks, blah blah blah repeat repeat repeat.  

The auctions are horrible. I don't like that they took the Evo bidding and removed the ability to outbid immediately after someone outbids you. Very little flexibility, and plans can be ruined by irrational bidders. Whoever is able, pretty much by whim, to get a province or two cheaply during the game seems to have an insurmountable advantage.

RATING: 8
Rated "8" after one play. Not as complex as Tigris and Euphrates, but more complex than Ra. This is more than an auction game. Sure, the theme is pretty thin, but this Knizia guy sure has a knack for making unique games. 

I really like how the board is wiped clean, except for the buildings. The second wave of bidding is pretty neat after the prior round of building. And the group "sacrifice" is pretty clever as well. It's "mathy," but I enjoyed it.

Update: after too much of a wait, played this a 2nd time. Yep, still a solid 8. Love the auctions in the 2nd phase, love the balance of trying to manage money. Fairly weak theme, but a very good game.

Update: well, finally got to play this with five players, and that is clearly the ideal count. Very solid game, and I can see various paths emerging for pulling out a victory. The five player experience has really sold me on this title.

RATING: 7
I've only played this once, and it's quite piece-y and rule-y for a first time through.  As with many other games of its weight (medium in my book), figuring out the relative importance of all the various ways to score is a toughie, especially without previous experience in the game.  The power card draws seem to pretty heavily tilt the success balance in one direction or another; getting multiple power cards with contradictory (or identical) scoring conditions can make one's power card investments useless, for instance.

The biggest problem I have with the game is how there's very little information written on power cards.  The instruction book had to be passed too often.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I enjoy the theme of this game, particularly the way the Nile floods in the middle of the game. Very flavorful.

Amun-Re feels very 'Knizia' to me. The way you are constantly needing to evaluate what things are worth...and the way that may change to different players slightly (or more than slightly) depending on other factors. The winner will be the person who was best able to find bargains and squeeze the most out of their actions...cumulatively over time. 

I used to think this game really needed 5 players to really shine. With less, the various bonus points cards conditions get harder to meet and the auctions for the second half are not as unpredictable as they could be (since some territories will not be in the mix). What DOES get less predictable is which bonus conditions you can fulfill, so purchasing cards during the first half is more of a gamble (with under 5 players). I think the game still works fine. It also may pressure someone to pay substantially more for a 2nd half province if they know that its their only chance to meet certain bonus conditions. Anyway, now I feel like it still works fine with less than 5, its just different. 



I suspect with more playings my rating may go up.

RATING: 5
Amun Re is a game that I thought was pretty snazzy when I first played it, but with the benefit of hindsight I can say that was only because I hadn't really played all that many games when this one came out.

Basically take a Knizia-style scoring system ala Ra, add several dashes of the unknown via  power cards, and serve. The main points of interest in this game are the auction for the region cards and the sacrifice at the end of the round. The auction is fairly novel, and I wouldn't mind seeing it reused in a new game. The sacrifice is quite important, and it can be a good source of income, points, and stuff if you plan ahead accordingly. That said, it's just an in the fist auction, and it's usually fairly apparent who is going to be sacrificing, and how hard.

I can't really explain why, but I find this game dull, dry, and unengaging. 

Simply doesn't stand out in my mind as doing anything particularly well...I wouldn't mind playing it again but only would if someone else needed a fifth.

Update: Homesteaders has re-used the auction mechanic and actually scales effectively (in addition to just being a more interesting game to me)...thus, Amun Re is now completely obsolete for me.

RATING: 5
This game has the honour of being the first title I ever played at a game convention after I 'got into the hobby' more or less officially; it is also the first genuine big box boardgame I bought for myself. Regrettably, Amon Ra suffered from a number of problems which might have otherwise stayed my hand, had I had the experience I now have. First of all, it's not a good game to play with 3 players, at 4 it's okay, and it's not until you play with 5 that you play 'as intended'. 5 players is a problem in my neck of the woods. Second issue: the fact that there are so many little rules and twists means that the people you play it with must be able to put up with the game for a number of times at least. So I need 4 other players with gaming experience. That's an even bigger problem.

Other than that it is a solid, if a little dry title with plenty of tiny agonising little twists, especially in the second half of the game when the presence of the pyramids and stones makes the right choice of province to buy and work not easy. You need players with a good intuitive sense of numbers and return on investments to play Amon Ra: the game will therefore not appeal to everyone. Amon Ra is not easy on newcomers: explaning the many little rules can be brutal; nor is it easy on experienced players: one mistake/upstart in your cashflow will probably cost you the victory as you don't have the time to make up for the loss. Which is of course the challenge. The little shortcomings made me decide to give Amon Ra a slightly lower rating that it otherwise would've gotten.

[b]Update[/b] Amon Ra is not a game which appears often at my table, and when it did the other day I ran into some issues which crystallised a few thoughts I'd been harbouring. My partner obtained, through sheer luck of the draw, so many bonus point power cards that it was more or less pointless to continue past the first era. (We did, though, and she won, as I'd expected.) I then noticed that there are decidedly good and bad cards. Even though a big chunk of the game is in working out when to use the right card, it is [i]very difficult[/i] to win without their point and money bonuses. The game doesn't really offer an easy alternative to transform money into VP: pyramids are really not that interesting because they don't result in major score [i]differences[/i]. (Hence the power of good scoring power cards.) With less than 5 players not all temple provinces may come out either, killing off that strategy quite effectively too; and with 5 players (ought to!) know better than to let someone get his hands on all temples. There are a few suggestions for card improvements floating around, and they certainly will help a little, but still I wonder whether it does any good for the game as a whole. It will still be a rather barren base game augmented by somewhat juicier cards.

All in all: to my current eyes, the design feels dated and limited, which, given the elaborate ruleset, is not a particularly good sign. I've downgraded the rating by 1 point.

[b]Update 2[/b] No more worrying when I will ever play this. Sad to see my first genuine multiplayer boardgame leave the collection... but sometimes a gamer's gotta do what a gamer's gotta do. Downgraded the rating by one point to reflect the final sentiment.

[COLOR=#990000]**[Español]**  El juego más duro de Knizia junto con el T&E.

Es un juego con dos tipos de subasta; una de ellas es la típica 1 2 3... y la segunda es una subasta a ciegas, la cual a pesar de las quejas de muchísima gente, hace que sea una absoluta genialidad por la consecuencias que conlleva; por ejemplo por la posible rebaja de costes en la compra de un mismo material comprado anteriormente.

El juego se adapta perfectamente con 3 o 4 personas pero es mejor que se juegue con 5 para que salgan las 15 provincias. Todo el mundo debería tenerlo, convéncete de ello jugando en www.spielbyweb.com[/COLOR]

RATING: 8
Good gamers euro style game.  Amun-Re is a fun euro game based on two ages of Egyptian history.  Players strive to erect pyramids, farm, and gain advantage and flexibility for future turns through money and card acquisition.  At the end of the first age, everything is wiped out except for the pyramids.  The second phase of the game starts and the players erect more pyramids, farm and work at gaining more cards and money.  Scoring is done twice in the game and the player with the most points wins.  Amun-Re is a good, fun euro style game with little down time.  It plays in less than two hours and is ideal with 4 or 5 players.  Also the bidding process for territories in the Egyptian empire is innovative and creates a good dynamic which players have to adjust to depending on how the other players play. This is a very good game for any gamers collection that likes semi easy fun euro style games.  This is one of my top 50 euro style games of all time.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

An interesting combination of an auction game (like Vegas Showdown) and an economic planning game.  It's been tough with 5 players - would like to try it with fewer.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Mechanically, Amun-Re doesn't feel particularly innovative. All of its singular parts - triangular auctions, income management, infrastructure development, blind bidding, and so on - have been used, and used well, in other designs. But it is extremely tightly constructed. The purchase mechanic magnifies the angst involved in making big moves, the sacrifice system creates an interesting tension and narrative, and the board control reset in particular works really well here to ratchet up the intensity of the later auctions.

There is a huge disparity in the relative strengths of the various power cards, though, adding an unfortunate element of luck into the mix; poor draws can kill an otherwise well-played game. The replayability also suffers as players become more experienced, especially the first half of the game; the relative values of provinces stabilizes and the auctions become much less interesting until the board reset. But in general it's a well-made game and the first few plays after you "get" the game are spectacular.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6

RATING: 8
The good doctor is back in Egypt with a meaty boardgame that has a lot to like about it.  Players play 2 "turns" - the Old Kingdom and the New Kingdom.  Each turn consists of purchasing via Evo-auction a province, then developing the province with farmers and pyramids.  It all costs money, so you have to concentrate on income also.  At the end of the first half, ownership is removed, yet the pyramids remain!  A bit of down time whenever a player takes power cards, which is exaggerated with more players, however this is clever stuff.

RATING: 6
A good Knizia game from his best period, but not one of the classics from that same period.  The auction mechanism is interesting, but the most interesting thing here is how the game resets itself halfway through apart from the pyramids, all of which remain.   The game has some parallels in its structure to Wallenstein/Shogun where you have 3 identical rounds, followed by scoring, then halftime, then do that again.   The scoring is a bit tight and a bit coarse.   Drawing the action cards feels pretty lucky as most of them aren't usually what you are looking for.   

I have some issues with the TMG production in the 2017 version.   The pyramids are the same color as the board, and therefore hard to see.  The double pyramids are still really annoying as you can't see the second pyramid from the back - it just looks like one.  The number of pyramid and stone bits is quite low, and you constantly have to make change, which you are doing all game long with the money cards too.   The auction cards could pop a little more off the board.   It definitely is a $30 game in a $70 box.

RATING: 9
Amun-Re is a good interesting game. The fun part of Amun-Re is when you bid for the land and the sacrifice. To figure out how much each land is worth to you and compare its worth for the other players is amazing. This can do deeper than your average board game.

At first, I would always lose at this game. I have many games played and havn`t won once in many years. Oddly enough, my last two plays are two wins...one point win in both cases. I guess it depends on who you play against.

I don't know why I often lose. I guess I can't handle my expenses in this game compared to other games I've played which has the auction/bidding mechanic. 

I still have fun when I play this game even if I do poorly. Though, I know I could have played better when I have a poor performance. I always look for ways to do better and calculate my expenses, hopefully for a win. 

Having two rounds in the game and the fact the pyramids stays in the 2nd round makes it very interesting on how you bid for land. More so than in the first round. Especailly if you have 3 pts cards in your hands at the start of the 2nd round. Lots of things to consider and think about. Lots of different possibilities.

Maybe there is a better way on how to spend your money more wisely if you want a chance to win. How much for the land your want? How much to sacrifice?

Lots of choices on how much you spend your money in Amun-Re.

Something that I enjoy more now than I did at the begining. The game has grown on me with more plays. A real treat!


11 plays since recording games played.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a highly regarded auction game played in two phases.  It kind of reminds me of Brass in that respect that some things get cleared off the board after the first phase and others remain in the second phase.  The difference is that the things that are left on the board in Amun-Re belong to no one and will be fought over in auction.  Amun-Re was the innovator in games that use the "you've been outbid so you have to bid on a different item" that's been found in Cyclades and Homesteaders.  I enjoyed that aspect of the game and the asymmetrical regions that players bid on.  I found the end game point cards and the randomness of the card to be of an annoyance to me.  Yes me, the card gamer, is complaining about card draw.  It was really annoying that after a person wins an auction that contains few/no farmer fields that I draw into a 9 farmer bonus point card or if I focus on getting the temples the, I draw no +/- 3 power cards.  Another disappointment in the cards is that you can go through the whole deck of cards and then be screwed out of getting point cards in phase two.  Overall, this is an interesting game, that could get better in my eyes with repeated plays but I'm not going to keep it, because with 5 people I would much rather play BSG, El Grande, or perhaps Game of Thrones.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[Auction/Action, Investment, Economic, Egyptian] {5P:10, 4P:6, 3P:4} Yet another Knizia design with mindbending gameplay and scoring. This time, players are Pharoahs, and work to build great pyramids and offer great sacrifices to the sun god Amun-Re. The theme isn't as well-integrated as it is in Euphrat & Tigris, but it still works well with the support of the excellent Hans im Gluck production. It follows an auction/action format similar to The Princes of Florence, with a couple of interesting twists. Amun-Re is less accessible than Euphrat & Tigris, but it grows on you with every play. *** UPDATE: Not as mindbending as I initially believed (see Taj Mahal and Modern Art for that) but still an excellent resource management and planning game. Stays a nine at the moment, though I doubt that it'll be a ten... *** UPDATE: Finally settled on an '8' for Amun-Re. It's a great game, one that I'll gladly play anytime, anywhere, but as far as auction/action goes it's a notch behind PoF, which remains the king of its game format on the strength of elegance and theme.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
[b]Duration:- [/b]:star::star::star::star::nostar:  [i]A touch rinse-and-repeat, but a good in-game arc makes up for this.[/i]
[b]Engagement:- [/b]:star::star::star::star::nostar:  [i]Continuous player involvement; precise gameflow.[/i] 
[b]Experience:- [/b]:star::star::star::star::star:  [i]Variable setup induces replyability whilst maintaining a fine strategic atmosphere.[/i]
[b]Presentation:- [/b]:star::star::star::nostar::nostar:  [i]Mouth-watering pyramids and stones in a thoroughly Egyptian backdrop; let down slightly by easily worn cards.[/i]

Another brilliant Knizia game; utilises the auction mechanism quite well.  The subtleties of scoring and sacrifice add to the tension which makes this game so palatable.

UPDATE 14AUG08:  After five years this game is still standing up so well it is becoming a classic favourite of mine; additionally it is also appreciated by just about everybody in our group - I am increasing the rating to a 9.

RATING: 9.5
One of the earliest games I own and probably still my favourite game.  I've fallen out of love with quite a few of my Knizia games over the years but not this one.

For me, this is the most rounded 5-player game I own.  It is best with 5 (it does work ok with 3 or 4) as there are 15 territories and these are all utilised over 3 eras in a 5-player game.   In games with fewer players some of the territories are left unused and although this means no two games will play out the same I find I prefer 5-players.

The Board graphics and components are excellent and create enough basic theme to avoid the 'pasted on' slurs.
I've often been asked what we are playing when in a public area such as a Cruise ship lounge.

I might have to pick up a second copy as we always take this one on holiday/vacation and it's starting to look a little aged! :)

RATING: 8
Take a great auction mechanism, constantly shifting goals depending on what the other players do, and a fair shot of double-guessing. Add in the typically multi-layered scoring opportunities, and you have one of Knizia's greats. The reset point at the halfway mark in particular looms over every action in the first half of the game because the pyramids you commission now might be the ones that guide your opponent to greatness later on.

No doubt some people will find this dry and mechanical, too, but from erecting pyramids through sacrifes to Amun Re and the harvests at the mercy of the Nile, all the elements make a lot of sense thematically.

Just played it again after a 3-year lay-off. It is still an excellent game!

RATING: 6
I'd been looking forward to playing Amun-Re, as Knizia's abstracts always stir me in the right way. Yes. Abstract. But, I enjoy his pasted-on themes regardless. Sadly, I was a bit let down. When I heard read the rules, I could not get a handle on what to do. There was a huge disconnect between all the scoring options. I feel Ra has a lot more symbiosis. Anyway, by the last round I was truly getting the game and actually felt it was a very tight game and an interesting approach to auctioning and purchasing. In that, I felt much warmer to it by the end and I think a second play would garner my favour a bit more. However, I can't remember the last time that I was so _bored_ watching other people's turns. Even with 4 players it took a bit of time to get back around, and watching other player's analyze their options was uninteresting to say the least. As such, I think there are games like this (even ones that Knizia has made) that have a lot more tension to them, even while you are waiting for your turn. I wouldn't refuse a game if suggested, but not one I'm really enamored with.

RATING: 6
Amun Re is a unique gaming experience.  Although the auction ultimately becomes the primary influence in the game, as it determines which areas people control, the implementation of power cards, plus the addition of the second dynasty (forcing everyone to relenquish power over previously controlled areas)makes the auctions play out most differently than in other auction based games.  Balancing your economy, and building between dynasties is key. The blind bidding element between each round, for the favor of the gods, is also added dimension that makes balancing ones decisions more difficult.  Knizia again creates a tense environment by making us want to do more than we can, but also adding in more things we have to eventually be aware of.  This game strikes a nice balance between long term strategy and quick decision making.  A great game, but it falters increasingly with fewer players than the maximum.

Update:  This downgrade in rating is largely due to the lack of interest this game garners with myself and fellow gamers in my group.  I have not played it in a while and that should say something.  The down slide could be temporary as it may eventually find it's way back to the table and inspire a lift, but until then ... down to a 6.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A good game, with a few options on your turn and a good bidding mechanism for areas, which keeps that part of the game interesting.

You can be limited in terms of points scoring depending on what cards you are able to collect (you can chose to gain cards as part of your turn, at a cost, but you are given them blind, and some give you scoring opportunities and others give other advantages).  You are able to adjust your strategy if you get appropriate cards early enough, but collecting cards later on can just lead to frustration.

There are ways around the cards, but sometimes it takes too much time or money and is no guarantee of getting what you need.  I find this aspect of it a bit too problematic for my liking.

RATING: 7
Interesting game, where the theme really relates to and enhances game play. One of Reiner Knizia's games that really works.

There is a lot going on in this game at all times.  There are many interesting and unique game mechanisms, so it takes a game or two to master.  Once you do though, it is a fun, fascinating game with the best Egyptian theme I've seen.

The steep learning curve and the long game length (90 minutes is definitely an understatement of game length) make it one that can't just be pulled out to play at any time.

RATING: 8.5
[b]Amun Re[/b] [i]Reiner Knizia 2003[/i]
A wonderful blend of some other game mechanics: bluffing, biding, majorities and some intricated ways to score ("a la Knizia"). Its nice auction mechanics are present in more modern games like Cyclades, Last train to Wendsleydale or Homesteaders. One of the best Knizia games, youll need experience to win at it. The only related luck factor are the special scoring cards but is not a big deal and no game will be decided just because of luck. It takes one hour and a half for experienced players and its only drawback is that its a game for just five players, no more, no less.

RATING: 7.5
We weren't able to play a complete game, just the first age, so I can't comment fully on the whole game. My initial impression from the mechanics and gameplay is good, however. I've always been a sucker for computer RTS games that involve gathering resources and building, and this game is similar in some respects to that model. When we added up victory points at the end of the first age, I was within striking distance of those ahead of me, but I'd also stretched myself thin on resources. One player, interestingly, focused heavily on farming and economy in that first age and was thus way behind on victory points but well ahead financially and in a position to take whichever province he wanted in the bidding round. It's a very intriguing game. Until I can play through to completion, I'll reserve my final judgment, but as of now, it's an appealing game with a nice level of complexity.

Amun-Re got a lot of play early, and I'm still happy to play it, but don't feel the urge to suggest it. Anyone who has basically mastered it will usually crush beginners, and so the fun isn't there if one player is better than everyone else. And after 8 games of this, I've yet to lose. It's the Puerto Rico problem. In theory, I like both games a lot. In practice, I won't suggest either one unless I find another player or two who can make the game challenging again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A fascinating game. Players aren't usually buying things; they are buying opportunities. They're buying fixer-upper properties in the hopes of scoring lots of points. Amun-Re uses a different auction system than Knizia's other games, what I think of as the "best offer" auction: bids increase according to pyramid scoring, and if you are overbid, you have to bid on something else. This makes players consider how much they are willing to pay at the outset because they may only get one shot.

THEN, after you play the first half of the game, there's another half where the board is almost fully reset, except players get to keep their cash and cards, and then they rebid on the properties they improved in the first half. This is just brilliant. There's also a collective blind bid to reach a certain threshold that is tense and awesome.

All told, this is just a fantastic game.

RATING: 8
Just don't find that I play this enough to rate it any higher than a 7. I think I am beginning to find that the scoring in some of Knizia's games, while well designed, seems a little convoluted. I do like several aspects of the game: the balance between scoring and building up income, the tension surrounding the race for the most pyramids, the extra actions that come from the sacrifice and how they can affect pyramid majorities.

Game feels empty without 5 players. One of the few 'best with full 5' games (PoF, El Grande) I have really felt I am disappointed to play with fewer than 5. Without 5 you don't use all of the territories and this can really change the game.

RATING: 9
Sixteen face-to-face plays, plus about twenty online. A rich, complex game that allows for a certain amount of strategic thinking without ever feeling heavy or draggy. It's bound to seem a little awkward and opaque at first, but once you've played a few times the mechanisms all click together and the game takes on a life of its own--like Ra, there's a weird feeling of bounce to the system where it seems like the players are participants in a larger story that's playing out on its own. Spooky!

Unlike some people, I think it plays just as well with four as it does with five. Three is a little weird because it can be much harder to pull off the power cards, but on the other hand it moves really quickly.

RATING: 8.5
Always exciting. Many ways to score and quite a lot of interaction. This has arguably more "flavour" and theme than any other Knizia game and doesn't feel dry at all. The game keeps exciting me after many, many plays. It might very well strike a more "ideal" balance between strategy and tactics (at least from my point of view) than any other game out there. I feel that I have a very good sense of the options available for strategic play, and yet I haven't quite "figured the game out" by identifying a set of key "ways to play" that would always work. I usually can't tell what my final scoring position will be until the very end, and yet the game doesn't feel "random". If a game can be "intuitive", Amun-Re certainly is.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Amun-Re is an area building game that masquerades as an auction game...or perhaps the other way around. Kiniza created something profound here. In Amun-Re, players auction to build up territories they control with various point scoring opportunities, but there are many decision points, offering twists and turns along the way. Money is the sole currency for all actions. Players must choose to use money to bid high for a location or use money to purchase point generation or use money to determine the nile level that decides what how much each item will score. Every choice a player commits to lessens their power to influence the others. Mix this with a unique bidding system where any player can force bids to increase for all or abruptly end the round, Amun-Re is a great system that is difficult to replicate. Card abilities can be random, but if everyone focus on drawing cards, much of the game is in everyone's control.

RATING: 7.25
I definitely enjoyed this on a first solo play but don't feel I have a good enough feel for it to give a fair rating yet.  Probably somewhere between 6-7, at a SWAG.

Updated after 2nd solo play: this is yet another interesting Knizia auction variant game, with a decent theme, but I simply don't like it as much as Taj Mahal.

Perhaps it's the two scoring periods, or the randomness of the important power card bonuses, or the fiddliness of the golden ratio bid values, but somehow this one seems to play longer, with less bang for the buck, and isn't even as pretty to the eye.

Definitely not a bad game, just not my favorite among the Knizia auction games, unless played with 5, where it shines better.

After 3rd play (with 5 players) raising this to a 7.25, but this is conditional on a full player count.

Donated 1st copy, obtained replacement.

RATING: 7
(03/2009) Interesting bidding mechanism, with category dominance for scoring, much like other Knizia games. The game drips with theme, as players build pyramids, till farmland, and make sacrifices to Amun-Re, only to see everything except the pyramids get wiped away due to the passage of time. Players then rebid to gain control of these pyramids to build more pyramids in a second "epoch", much like in Ra. I rate it a "7".

Update (02/24/11) 3 plays: the game is slowly getting traction in our group, as players get familiar with the rules and fascinating gameplay. Players competitively bid for various kingdoms, much like trying to buy primo beachfront property, trying to get the best deal. This is classic Knizia: "You don't want to bid too low, but you don't want to bid too high; you want to bid just right..." Goldilocks-style bidding. This also applies to spending money to improve the kingdoms with pyramids and farmers. Because of the progressively increasing costs of power cards, farmers, and stone (to build the pyramids), you can't go all out, because then you would not have enough money to make a worthy sacrifice to Amun-Re. If you win the sacrifice bid, Amun-Re will shower you with gifts to possibly take the lead in one or several scoring categories. If frugal or stingy, you can try to pull everyone else down, but also not get much in terms of progress. Interesting implementation of the "Prisoner's  Dilemma". 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Un diseño de los mejores de Knizia que, una vez más, está ambientado en una civilización antigua cualquiera. El diseño ofrece cantidad de opciones estratégicas: elegir las provincias que te gustaría quedarte en la subasta, ir a por granjeros (que dan dinero) o ir a por pirámides (que dan PV´s), además de las jugadas "chupi" que dan las cartas. Añade a todo esto la gracia que otorgan los dos tipos de subasta: una con incrementos forzados (1,3,6,10...) y otra "ciega" que permite obtener recursos por poco dinero además de determinar cuánto produce tu provincia. Como se puede ver hay mucho en lo que pensar: cuánto pujar, dónde pujar, que desarrollar... Me gusta la sensación que provoca esta mecánica: la de intuir la respuesta de los demás jugadores en la subasta. No tengo claro si me gusta o no los bandazos que pega el juego (no sabes si jugarlo en plan "optmización" o en plan "intuición") PROS: La segunda parte del juego (cambia totalmente el valor de las provincias al conservarse las pirámides en ellas y desparecer los granjeros). Muchas formas de puntuar "a la Knizia": por pirámides, por granjeros, por mayorías de pirámides, por bonus especiales de las cartas, por oro... Esto ocasiona muchas estrategias. CONS: Es dificil encontrar la estrategia óptima en las primeras partidas. No es un juego para montar y jugar. No jugarlo con menos de 5 jugadores.

[ENG]

One of the best Knizia´s desings that its setting is about an ancient civilization. The design shows a lot of strategic options: choosing the provinces you like during the auction, going for farmers (and increase the money level) o going for pyramids (they give you VP´s), futher, there are a quite pretty cool gambles when you´re using cards. And more, becouse there are two types of auctions: first of them with fixed bids (1,3,6,10...) and the second is a kind of "blind auction" where you can obtain a bunch of goods using little money. As can you see, there is much to think about: how much bid, where to bid, what you need to develop. I do love the "intuition feeling", trying to gess what´s the another player thinking about his/her bid in the auction. I´m not sure if I like the wide variations of the game (I don´t know if I´d play it "optimizely" or "intutively"). PROS: the second part of the game (it changes the provinces´ value becouse the pyramids remain there and farmers don´t). There are a lot of ways to obtain VP´s "ala Knizia": pyramids, farmers, firs majority of pyramids, cards special bonuses, gold... this circumstance generates a lot of strategies. CONS: really difficult to find the optimal strategy during the first games. You should play it only with 5 players.

RATING: 8
I really like games with farming themes. I've played Amun-Re game 40  times now and it keeps getting better, and with the player aids I've developed now I can teach this game fairly fast. Fascinated how Knizia is now designing games not so much on mechanics, but how he wants people to interact. Each round is a little script --you've got the besting of the province auction, the intentness of the build, then the tension and anticipation of the closed fist sacrifice, with the cheering and jeering by the players who prevail. Interesting that after Hans im Glück accepted the design they required Reiner to add the Power Cards as added spice.

RATING: 8.5
Not an overly deep game by any means here but a strategy title all the same. I do find that the strategies are a little too obvious concerning the provinces although the power cards can impact your decisions. I can see why others have a quibble with the power cards, the game other than this is quite balanced (as usual for a Knizia) so the cards can really be the deciding factor with players of equal skill and it should be a close game unless you failed miserably in the auctions which can happen, especially with 5. Knizia certainly likes a little luck/chance in his games and this is it for this game but these are definitely not the "only" factor in determining the outcome, for example you can exert some control in the auctions to bend things your way, same with the sacrifice, etc. The 2 different auctions are quite good and most of the strategy comes in right here, I like that you only get 'one shot' at a province so you "go big or go home". I was surprised how tight the money was in this game which really adds to the tension. 

This was a fun game that needs more plays with more players, I see people saying it is only really good with 5 people and I think I can see why.... 

[u]UPDATE:[/u] Another FTF game but with 5 players, much better than with three but rating sticks. We still didn't see the sacrifice get over the half way mark (into the higher numbers) except once. 

[u]UPDATE:[/u] Finally seen the sacrifice go up to 4 but it all seems too predictable as the only time it will happen is at the end when money is more in abundance.  5 players seems the only way to go for me. Overall a fun strategy game, just be prepared to have the cards you get dictate your strategic path. 

[u]UPDATE:[/u] 8 => 8.5 This game grows on me just a tiny bit with each play, up to about 10 plays now. Its very enjoyable and would be rated even higher less the luck of the draw which can (but wont always) determine a winner in a close game with all good players, most games are close. Most likely my final rating

RATING: 7
The good doctor is back in Egypt with a meaty boardgame that has a lot to like about it. Players play 2 "turns" - the Old Kingdom and the New Kingdom. Each turn consists of purchasing via Evo-auction a province, then developing the province with farmers and pyramids. It all costs money, so you have to concentrate on income also. At the end of the first half, ownership is removed, yet the pyramids remain! A bit of down time whenever a player takes power cards, which is exaggerated with more players, however this is clever stuff.

RATING: 10
I have played the game A LOT and in my opinion the game is, quite simply, marvellous. It blends various clever game mechanics, with the economic game feeling almost completely separate from, yet still integrated with the VP game. At first sight, the luck of the power card draw seems significant (and there is luck involved, to be sure), but with experience I find all the power cards useful, and extremely well balanced in their power. The game offers gutwrenching decisions almost continuously (but especially so in the province auction phase). Many German-style games are very tactical, ie. you can do reasonably well with only short-term planning, but Amun-Re is extremely strategic, so you *have* to think quite far ahead in order to do well. Highly, highly recommended. One of Knizia's best games, if not THE best.

RATING: 6.5
2016-05-29

Initial Rating: 6.0 (May 2016)

Nowadays, I expect almost nothing from Mr. Knizia´s games, so when I played Amun-Re I wasn’t expecting much but I was pleasantly surprised. However this game was designed on 2003 so it seems I do like his earlier/heavier designs. 

The theme is ok and it feels thematic for a Knizia game but, in reality, the theme is rather thin.

The rules are very easy to learn and teach, you can do it under 18 minutes. Amun-Re´s components are great for such an old game and they look gorgeous on the table.

Amun-Re has bidding as its main mechanism and I love bidding games. The decisions are interesting, tense and non-obvious (how much to bid for each card, how many workers/pyramids/cards to purchase, how much to bid for the temples). It also has lots of player interaction. I love how the game resets itself at midpoint, so the players need to re assess their strategy and position (somewhat reminiscent of Brass). You really need to plan ahead if you want to win. 

Best with 5 players.

On the bad side, I feel the luck of the draw with the power cards can be more than I’m comfortable with. I need to play more times to check if this is indeed an issue for me but drawing scoring cards that you already accomplished or that are impossible to accomplish isn’t very fun or fair.

Bottom line, I need to play Amun-Re more times. For now I’m keeping the game and perhaps it can get an upgraded rating from me. One of the best Knizia´s games I have played.

Current Rating: 6.5

RATING: 8
Amun-Re is a nice collection of good ideas. There are 15 provinces on the board. Each turn a player wins a province at auction, then players buy power cards (which give bonuses in certain game phases), farmers (which provide income) and bricks to build pyramids (which give points). Players then bid money as a sacrifice to the god Amun-Re. The total value of the sacrifice from all players combined primarily indicates how much income the farmers produce on that turn. Players can drive the total down by stealing from Amun-Re (if they don't have a lot of farmers, say), but those players miss out on the gifts bestowed to those who have sacrificed the most. Then income is determined. This happens 3 times until every province has been purchased. The first three turns are called the "Old Kingdom" - after which pyramids, temples, and power cards are scored - then the board is cleared of everything except pyramids and it starts again with the "New Kingdom".

Knizia built his reputation mostly on his innovative auction mechanics and Amun-Re is right there with the best. Amun-Re has two kinds of auctions: a set value auction for provinces which works great, and a collective blind-bid auction for sacrificing to Amun-Re, where the bids are all totaled together for a combined value that applies to all players equally. Some players are interested in keeping the sacrifice low, so landing a high sacrifice forces some players to throw in a lot of money. The game is not difficult by any means, but there are enough decision points to keep players engaged. I'm torn between scoring it a low 9 or a high 8. Like most Knizia games, there is enough randomness in the power cards to keep this solidly in the medium-weight range. I don't mind the randomness of the power cards, but the deck does get cycled through a few times and get less interesting as the options get more limited each time through. It's a bit messy, which keeps me from putting this with Knizia's top two games - Ra and T&E - but Amun-Re is a thoroughly enjoyable game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
In Amun-re, players preside over two generations of development in different regions of ancient Egypt. Provinces are auctioned off, and from there, it's a matter of turning arable land to advantage (building income) and building pyramids. You are rewarded for complete sets of pyramids, having the most pyramids on one side of the Nile, settling only on one side of the Nile, or settling all in the north or all in the south. That's all well and good, but the bit of genius tucked away in Amun-re is that each player can affect the income of his or her opponents. The turn ends with a "sacrifice" in which all players simultaneously reveal any amount of tribute. If they pay in enough as a group, harvests are good (high income). If they are stingy or even steal from the tribute (yes, you can steal from the tribute), harvests are poor. This works because not all provinces are equally productive, so some players have a higher potential income but a higher incentive to worship Amun-re as well. Very cool.

Update: A second play convinces me that the 8 rating for this game is on solid ground. Undoubtedly a classic. The last great game for Knizia; he's had a seven year drought following it. I'm not sure there's anything in the game that will raise it to a 9 for me, but I'd be happy to play it just about any time. Requires a lot of skill. A very intense game; make a few mistakes and you will creep along in last place. 

2nd Update: We don't get Amun-Re to the table that often, but this is an extremely resilient 5-player game. Very tough push-pull going on from start to finish. Perhaps the strongest point here is that someone who is slightly behind can "play their ass off" and make up a little ground here and a little there... very satisfying.

RATING: 3
Knizia's auction system is great and the game as a process is good. A brilliant auctioning system is, however, overshadowed by perfectly unbalanced cards. You can win through skilled playing, but most probably you'll need a couple of bonus scoring cards even then. What bothers me a lot more is that you can play brilliantly, but still lose the game because of unfortunate draws. I don't know many other games where I'd noticed this could be possible. So, a 90-minute game is actually reduced into a 1-minute luckfest in the end where drawing the right power cards is the deciding factor. I've once ended second in a 5-player game where the winner won by 12 points. We'd have had equal amount of points without his bonus scoring cards while I drew a total of 15 cards during the game without getting any bonus scoring card. Also, the farmers always suck which makes it a little boring. Just go for the camels if you're willing to win.  Stupid.

I love the mid-game switch, and I love the offerings, which are two weird, but interesting things that make the game different from everything else.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A game involving auctioning off of provinces, then earning points for various activities including developing of those provinces with pyramids. It is a fun game with an interesting twist that comes in the second half. For the second half of the game, a new age begins and all of the provinces are re-auctioned before continuing play. This means that players may benefit in the second half of the game from improvements made by another player in the first half. It’s interesting and challenging to balance earning points in the first age vs. the potential benefit an opponent may get for those improvements in the second age (and vs. saving up money to ensure you can buy key provinces for the second age).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7

RATING: 9
Excellent strategic auction and action game. Very nice components! The random selection of the provinces to be auctioned in each round ensures replayability. The drawing of the power cards might appear to introduce a high degree of luck, but each card has its value and appropriate use during the course of the game. Your strategy should be based on which cards you have or might get in the future (take in account the conditions to be fulfilled for the 3P bonus power cards). Try to control the province with the highest number of piramides on one of the sides of the Nile. Also the temple provinces combined with a high sacrifice can be quite rewarding. And of course, ... to be able to do all that you have to earn money via farmers or camels.

RATING: 9
The fixed number of rounds helps make the game a very fun but also concise experience.  There is a good deal of player interaction in spite of the individual resource development.  The game also improves with the skill of the players, especially their ability to evaluate the provinces available.

Missing provinces can randomly affect power card goals when playing with fewer than five players.  To help counteract this, you can choose and reveal the missing provinces at the start of the game, but a full complement of five is still the best situation.

RATING: N/A
New Release in March 2017

Each player wants, as pharaoh, to build the most pyramids. To accomplish this, he must first acquire a province, where he can trade and farm. With his profits, he can buy new provinces and building stones to erect pyramids. For all his actions, the player must make clever use of his power cards, and always offer appropriate sacrifices to Amun Re. Players must always keep their eyes on the goal of the building of the eternal pyramids or risk falling behind in points.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I think this game is over-hyped.  I'm usually a fan of Knizia games but this one falls short for me.  As an auction game with a good deal of bidding, winning at it really comes down to the deck of power cards and who manages to draw luckiest, that is, whoever draws cards "MOST appropriate" to their circumstances will win every time.  I've played it many times and winning or losing "always" comes down to the lucky card draws.  But, this is a BOARD game isn't it?  The mechanisms are great though.  Evaluating what to bid on each territory is very interesting and I like the auctions.  The most consistent way to play seems to be to build as many blocks while buying farmers & cards and hope to get luckier than the next person.  The game does indeed have a great production quality and a really nice presentation, but I just can not "overlook" the fact that this game is really disguised as a "Draw The Right endgame Power Cards".  There is not other randomn/luck factor in the game so it essentially all hinges upon that one stack of cards and what other players discard.  I'd rather play a card game or something in half the time, or if I like these types of auctions then I'd suggest Vegas Showdown to be played.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This game really is much more than an auction game although it centers around auctioning regions within ancient Egypt.  Each region will often provide different setups that will either aid in earning money for future purchases, building blocks for pyramids, farmers, etc as well as temples for scoring.  There is quite a few little details to keep an eye on to make sure one person doesn't get a monopoly on temple regions, etc.  Sacrifices to Amun-Re also give opportunity to determin income per farmer as well as grant opportunity to gain extra actions.  It definitely takes one play just to see the basic interworkings of the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I really enjoyed the theme and the auction mechanic.  There are multiple strategies to pursue (temples, farmers, mission cards), so the game offers good replayability.

RATING: 8
Amun-Re is a strange, Lewis Carrol twist on the standard systems game (ie. Puerto Rico, Princes of Florence, Caylus and the like). You have to invest in territory and infrastructure (farmers and pyramids) in order to establish income and scoring, only to have it blown up half way through the game *and* leave the pyramids for someone else to use later. No other game dares to have this kooky an income mechanism, and it works beautifully. The sacrifice is a weird informed-groupthink exercise, and the auction system is old hat now (see Vegas Showdown and Homesteaders), but in 2003 it was a platypus.

Somehow, the good Dr. Knizia has managed to avoid making Amun-Re feel like a jangly mess of mechanics, though. Where games like Caylus and Power Grid feel like a tangled mess, Amun-Re powerss along like a British roadster. I can't point to the difference with any confidence, but it is a question I will continue to study.

I will concede that the Power Cards can inject an awkward level of luck sometimes. While bad draws are not that big a deal, since you can cycle cards if you want to, it sucks when your opponent gets a magical run of useful cards. This is a small blemish, though, and one that I am willing to tolerate on occasion in order to enjoy the rest of this gaming symphony.

While I can't quite rank Amun-Re as highly as I do Race for the Galaxy or Princes of Florence, it is still an excellent heavy euro, and one I will happily play at any time.

RATING: 8
Rating after only one game and maybe it wasn't the right crowd for this game. This a dry, mathematical game. You count a LOT of things. In fact, you almost always do math. I really like that... but my fellow gamers not so much. 

This is not an easy game to explain at first. Each phases has its quirk and the scoring is extremely varied. But after an example round everyone should be able to understand.

I really like the economic of the game. You have to get your "cash-engine" rolling or else you won't be able to do nothing. I like that each resources of the game is equally important: Farmer for cash, Block for VP and cards for some wildness. Each province are not created equal and on the first game it is not easy to see which province are best. I guess it comes with experience.

Update:

Years later and this still gets played at least a few times a year. The bidding system that we now see in Vegas Showdown, Homesteaders, and even Peloponnes started here; but in a harsher twist in the "cannot bid in the same location once outbid".

The more the merrier but I've never been totally convinced that you have to play with max players for this to be a good game.

RATING: 7
An interesting and quite cut-throat bidding game with an element of luck. Sometimes the cards work for you, sometimes against.

One thing Knizia seems to do well is balance open information with randomness. I have some issues with the components - the bricks and bidding lozenges are a bit odd and the Pharaoh token is hopeless, plus the cartoonish graphics don't seem to fit the seriousness of the theme. Maybe Menzel should've done this one rather than Vohlwinkel? - but, otherwise, this is a great game that does a good job of hiding the winner right until the last moment.

If it weren't for the component issues, plus the fact that a few of my group already own it, I'd definitely buy this.

RATING: 8
If you like a bidding game you'll like this game.  You bid for different provinces in Ancient Egypt.  Some provinces are good for farming and others are good for building pyramids.  At a certain point of the game everything will be removed (except pyramids) and players will once again bid for provinces.  After bidding for provinces players will use their money to buy famers and construct pyramids.  The amount of money you will make will depend on how many farmers you have and how much money was sacrificed to Amun Re.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Amun-Re is a good game whose most important feature seems to be the blind auction that determines harvests and allows players to win additional resources. In Amun-Re, it's a little too easy to fall way behind because of one mistake (getting cash-strapped, choosing the wrong province) but this is because the duration of the game is a short 6 rounds. This is definitely a game where the time-value of resources plays a prominent role. 

I don't find the luck element introduced by the Power Cards to be a flaw; few games with cards *don't* have that kind of randomness. However, the role played by table position, in tie-breaking and bidding, can be annoying.

Also: Before you play, make sure all players agree (either way) on whether or not Bidding Blockade may be played out-of-turn. The rulebook isn't clear on the matter. The game plays better if this is allowed, but a lot of players say it must be played pre-emptively, making the card very weak.

RATING: 6
Game in a Nutshell: Auction for available properties that have their own unique bonuses, then compete to improve your lands better then your opponents. Improve in different ways and score points in different ways.

Played as: 4 player

Pros: The separation between old age then starting all over in the new age was interesting, as was the auctioning for areas at the beginning of each round.
 
Cons: Outside of those two pieces of mere interest, nothing else felt exciting or original. Just an average pick how you want to score your points, specialize, and crunch numbers like it's nobodies business. Probably the ugliest game I've played in that visually, the layout doesn't make the game very intuitive.

Other: Ok, ok. This is all initial impression after a single play. I'm sure I'll be giving it another try especially after I've read the rules, (which hopefully will help me better visualize the game).

Best Use: Need a brain-burn for 4 or 5 people.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
At its heart, Amun-Re is yet another resource conversion game: you're taking gold and turning it into farmers, provinces, bricks, and cards, which combine with each other into more gold, more of each other, and--ultimately--points. No matter how challenging these games might be, I typically don't find them all that intriguing, but Amun-Re has been an exception. Its nuances have been surprisingly counter-intuitive for me, and I seem to love games with a dramatic "board sweep" at the half (e.g., Brass). Amun-Re probably benefits from a dedicated group of five, and I bet it was excellent online.

However, there are some downsides. First, it takes quite a bit a little longer to explain than I'd prefer. (I pulled off a quick explanation once by skimping on the Power Cards, just pointing to the last page of the rules to use as a reference. Big mistake.) For the uninitiated, turns can seem a bit repetitive, making the game feel long. You'll want exactly five players, which can be a tough number to hit regularly. Finally, despite Amun-Re's advantages over your typical spreadsheet game, there's only so far I can go with this type of game.

Hopefully, Amun-Re will get some traction with a regular group of players. It could go far under the right conditions, but if it's just coming out with new players every time, it'll probably hit the trade pile within the year, especially since others in my gaming circle own it.

A bit disappointing. I really like the style of auction presented here, but just like Cyclades, the rest of the game built around it is pretty mediocre. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Amun-Re is a game with many faces. Is it an auction game, where your skills in valuating land and outbidding opponents gives you the edge? Or is it a building game, where your skills in managing your economy and improving your assets tip the balance in your favour? Or is it a conflict game, where you choose actions that benefit you and hurt the others? Well, Amun-Re is all this!

As a dynasty leader in the Old Kingdom of Egypt, you start by bidding for provinces. Do you want to build wealth through farming or trading? Do you want to acquire "safe" victory points by building pyramids or try your luck by buying unknown power cards? The choice is yours but beware, your success depends on all players' actions!

It is namely the god Amun-Re that brings fortune (good farming) or misfortune (good trading) to Egypt and his mood depends on the total amount that the dynasties sacrify to (or steal from) him. Your grand plans for your provinces will come to nothing if you don't take into account your competitors' plans.

So what happens if your land does prosper? Alas, the Old Kingdom falls apart and the New Kingdom rises. Everything your forefathers have built is doomed to vanish with one exception - the mighty pyramids. However, your tenderly nurtured provinces may now be acquired by someone else and it is not until the end of the New Kingdom that the worthy Pharaoh may claim the victory.

RATING: 7
I have fallen in love with this game.  The great blend of strategy and tactics makes for a deep, interesting game each time.  There is so much to learn and know about Amum-Re that I keep coming back to it for more.  I especially like how the values of all the provinces change as the game progresses and depending upon how they come up for auction together.

I know some people complain about the power cards, but since you draw so many of them over the course of the game, it really seems to even out.  That is, assuming you remove the overbid on a province card, as this one is useless.  I will play this one with either 3, 4, or 5, as I enjoy the game in all its forms, but it is best with 5.  I came to this one late, but I'm so glad that I picked it up.

RATING: 7.5
Overall comment: an auction type game that requires the player to manage their money in different ways from spending to giving it away.  The bidding is unique in gaming and where you are in the turn order is crucial to winning especially with 3 players.

Ratings:
Initial(wow factor):7 (expected a more interesting looking game and the pieces(ie. amun-re for example) seemed kind of cheap)
Family: 7 (sometimes requested)
Game group: 8 (all in the group will play but the game has has a sameness to it the last few times)
AP rating: 5 (some delays in the play)
Solo play: not yet developed

When purchased: 2007
Recommend expansions: none available
Age range: 10+
Luck factor: 2
Play time: 2 hours

Reason purchased: Geek analysis

Pros: offers a different take on the auctioning mechanic and puts the player in complete control of the outcome, the offering is a tricky action that ultimately sets up the next round
Cons: not much variety in the gameplay and the second epic can often be won by focusing on a single province which, when this is won by the player, often makes it difficult for others to overcome it.  This is as long as it wasn't overpayed for.

Recommendation: a good medium weight game that offers some strategic decisions, mostly with regards to cash flow, but does not overtax the mind in the process.  No big numbers here. It is a good game played in moderation.
Replay rating: slightly below average
Game value (plays/cost): Marginal (requests for play have fallen off recently due to the nature of the last 3 games played which seemed to be scripted)

RATING: 6.5
Highly anticipated release from Knizia in 2003. Players bid on provinces, buying power cards, placing farmers and build temples in ancient Egypt. Each round a sacrifice in money is made to Amun Re to determine how much further actions you get. It's a bit long, since if you make a mistake early, you will be out of the whole game. Some luck on the draw of the power cards (some give VPs, some give farmers, some give bonuses, etc). I would play it, but I prefer Knizia's other designs such as Taj Mahal or Euphrates and Tigres. This game also plays best with five players.

RATING: 5
Long before 'Legacy' games were a thing, Reiner Knizia created Amun-Re; essentially a short, bland auction game, followed by another short, bland auction game where players are now bidding on the partially developed holdings from the first game. A two-part Egyptian-themed Legacy game, if you will.

I'm not really a fan of auction games, nor am I a fan of any kind of serialized game, be it an RPG or a Legacy title, so the innovation of Amun-Re didn't excite me at all.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A really fun auctioning game.  I liked how the auctioning numbers were written on the cards and had a high number that could not go higher.  Along the nile you may be trying to get all your locations on the east, or west, or the biggest pyramids, but to own one you need to win the auction.  After winning two auctions you can build on that spot and use it's abilities.  Half way through the game you will score points, than re-auction every spot, possibly giving all your hard work to someone else, which is fine because you may want to take advantage of another spot anyway for the latter half of the game.

Not sure if all I wrote makes sense, but the game is a good auctioning game with secret objectives and lots of room to change and alter your strategy.

RATING: 9
Another one of Knizia's best. The theme fits well with the gameplay, and the many interlocking mechanisms seem to interlace with the theme. That these mechanisms are broken into their own sections makes the game feel much simpler than it really is. Amun-Re is part auction, part building, (part deduction, for advanced player), part brinksmanship. Mainly, though, it is a wonderfully brain-burning money management game. Well worth playing again and again.The game's replay value comes from the fact that the mechanisms affect each player in slightly different ways, based on the provinces one holds and the cards in hand. After around 40  plays I still do not enough of a handle on all of them to consistantly win.An excellent game in my book, and its heart is much lighter than a feather.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
規則:
*放置圖版，並放置對應的資源在對應區，錢幣卡分類擺放，奉獻版塊開著放上面，幫助卡洗勻(每人先發一張建築幫助卡)，省分卡每有一個玩家就拿三張，之後全部洗勻
*每個玩家拿一個顏色米寶，分數標記放0，每人拿20圓，每人在拿一張盜賊卡，隨機起始拿標記
*遊戲進行兩個時代，每個時代有三輪，每輪四個階段，每個時代結束會記分
1.競標階段 抽等同人數的省份卡放置在對應圖版位置，且同時放置該區的競標獎勵(石頭錢幫助卡)，玩家輪流放一個工人在省分卡上競標格，要放有人的要比他高，輪到你時如果你放的被標過則要移動到其他的標(除非使用賄絡幫助卡)，競標階段直到每個人都有標到一個省份
2.市場階段 照順位可買幫助卡/農夫/石頭，價格第一個一圓，每多一個多一圓，三種分開算，幫助卡可買張數跟你占的省份旁標示有關(多個選最大不累加)，農夫跟石頭買了要馬上放
*版塊上的田每個可以放一個農夫，農夫幫助卡的農夫可放任意省份無視農田
*當省分有三顆石頭換成一個金字塔，原本就有金字塔的換成兩層，建築幫助卡可讓你用兩顆蓋一個金字塔一次
3.貢獻階段 暗出金錢卡，如果是出盜賊卡則改從銀行拿三圓，加總所有金額的總合，拿對應數值的奉獻版塊，然後貢獻值照大到小拿3/2/1/1/1個獎勵(幫助卡/農夫/石頭)出盜賊的沒獎勵，平手比順位，第一的同時拿起始玩家標記
*藍色的幫助卡可一起跟著出增加貢獻值(但是不算在個人貢獻值)，順位在輪到你時在選擇要加還減
4.收獲階段  照順位，省份有礦的拿，駱駝的要看貢獻版塊是否有駱駝可拿，每個農夫根據貢獻版塊拿錢
*寶藏卡出時選一個省份不拿該區收入改拿8圓，大收獲指定一個省份每個農夫+1圓
*每個階段不能出兩張一樣的幫助卡，每張幫助卡可棄掉當1圓
*每個時代結束 每個省份的寺廟拿分，每個省份你的一個金字塔一分，你省份裡最少金字塔的每座3分，尼羅河兩岸各選一個省份最多金字塔的該玩家拿5分(平手比石頭在坪手都拿)，滿足黃色幫助卡的拿3份
*記完分後，場上農夫清空，工人回手，省份牌拿走洗勻
*第二個時代結束額外記分，手上錢照順位拿6/4/2分，平手都拿但沒下個名次，之後最高分獲勝平手比金字塔數，在比石頭
**專家規則**
*省份牌改為起始每人發三張，每輪每人同時出一張
