RATING: 9
Reiner takes on another Egyptian themed auction game! Ra is really just a trick-taking game with some interesting ClipArt. Maybe Amun-Re is really the game he meant to make with Ra. Or maybe Knizia just really likes the time period. In any case, this is Ra 2.0, as if the designer just added one more hour of gameplay for lulz. While Ra is light and fun when it comes to an auction game, Amun-Re presents a beautiful map which deepens strategies and makes the game thematic and rich. Now, we're pharaohs who buy provinces, pyramids and prestige, competing to own the most profitable and prominent lands. Also added to your pain is the sacrifice to Ra every round to determine how much money your provinces stand to make. But not every province is made equal: some provinces need the sacrifice to Ra to be just a little less which makes them money and no one else. There are also endgame bonuses which you can earn, based off your province's positions on the map. All this can sound complicated and compared to Ra, it is. But I had so much fun playing this one! I loved the auction mechanic which let me see who was positioned where, I loved the action cards which "broke" the game in interesting little ways, I even loved that the game was divided into two "ages" where the same provinces you spent the game building up and improving might not come back in the second half! It's a great game and am so glad I own a copy!

RATING: 9
Amun-Re is one of Knizia's masterpieces. It is a beautiful and deeply strategic game with a consistently realized theme.  

The phases affect each other in multiple ways with lots of small feedback mechanisms.  I love the tension of the auctions. The key idea is that there is a battle going on between two dissimilar groups: the traders and the farmers. These groups are balanced in strength even though there are more farming interests than trading. Be sure to choose the winning side! You can't win by trying to be both.

After many more games it appears there is [i]lots[/i] going on that I did not see at first.  It is far more than traders vs. farmers—although that is a key theme.  Everything in Amun-Re has to be evaluated in relation to everything else, and the values change according to what stage in the game that you get it. 

BTW, Amun-Re scales well. There is a myth here on the geek that it needs five to be good. That's baloney. It is every bit as good with four, and quite playable with three.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Amun-Re is a bidding game in which there are multiple auctions--each player will get one of the provinces up for bid each round.  The provinces all provide different bonuses, and scoring cards in your hand may make some provinces better based on their location or their attributes, so provinces are worth different amounts to each player.  Bid amounts are fixed to triangular numbers, making it really important to try to figure out what others will bid, as going even one spot ahead of another player can get pricey.  The game is augmented by a building phase also based on triangular numbers, preventing people from saving up and doing too much in one turn.  This is one of my favorite auction games, there's just so much player interaction and so much to think about.

(5+ plays)

RATING: 8
One of my favorite auction games. Evaluating the different provinces is really interesting, as their values change over the course of the game depending on one's particular strategy and set of scoring cards. The bidding system works wonderfully to force careful valuation as well as assessment of other players' values. 

I haven't played enough to have a good sense of how much depends on lucky draws of the right power cards, but there do seem to be good ways to ameliorate poor draws, such as focusing money not on winning particular provinces, but on building sets of pyramids or aiming for most pyramids on both sides.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Excellent Knizia auction game. This is one of my very favorite games to play with experienced players. Sadly, it can be frustrating to play with people less skilled or experienced. Amun-Re requires calculations to find the best play. The question that the game asks of you is: 'Considering the optimum moves for the other players, what is my best course of action?' Finding your way through all the intricately interconnected aspects of the game, estimating expected values, and solving this constantly shifting puzzle can be extremely satisfying; winning feels like a true victory, one that you had to work for.

However, one player giving away a province too cheaply, messing up the sacrifice, or getting into an ill-advised fight, can throw off the entire balance. And when you've just racked your brain to find that 1 or 2 coin edge, seeing another province go for 20 coins less than it should, is not fun.

That makes Amun-Re a bit of an odd duck. While I normally play games for the social interaction, I'd rather play this online, and would perhaps even prefer playing against AIs. So, a game that breaks when people are being, well, people? It's not really the game's fault: chess is also only fun against experienced players willing to think.

Highly recommended if you have a group of 5 (or 4) serious gamers, willing to work their brains. But if even one of the players isn't in the mood for something heavy, do yourself a favor and find another game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
First session with 5 which I personally found slowed the pace, increased the luck of getting desired power cards and made evaluation of each players vp status and intentions (during bidding and sacrificing) alittle foggy. Interesting mixture of mechanics and elements to consider, but feels alittle convoluted, though game sequence in Amun Re isn't repetitive as in Taj Mahal. Now played with 4, and more convinced that this is a 3 player game for easier assessment, and more skill involved. Finally played with 3 which made the provinces interestingly varied and game pace good. Amun-Re has some elements of Princes of Florence that I enjoy: the enforced pressure of matching conditions to get VPs and the bonus card and most money declaration at game end. In some ways, Amun-Re does it better than PoF: play is focussed centrally(rather than peripherally with individual playmats) making assessments easier; the "hare & tortoise/evo" bidding instils a tense and dramatic personality. I'm finding the game play and components provocative of the theme, so I'm charmed to play again. Just not sure if it will captivate the non-gamers I often play with.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

-

RATING: 9
Played this once and loved how the pyramids make the provinces extremely different in value in the New Kingdom (and often worth bidding up...making hoarding money for the New Kingdom a viable strategy). I had a lot of fun with this one...was way out in front after the Old Kingdom but settled for a tie for 3rd in a 5-player by game end. Hard to see who is really out in front due to the cards. Also thought the sacrificial bidding mechanic was neat (you can steal from the collection plate if you feel evil!)

Edit: A couple more plays and listening to the Metagamers' strategy podcast have elevated my rating to a 10. This game has the perfect mix of strategy and chaos for my taste and I can't recommend it enough.

Update: The power cards are slightly too swingy for this to maintain a 10 rating. Also, it needs an experienced group. Knocked it down a point.

RATING: 8
My obsession with Knizia is getting a little out of hand, and Amun-Re was one of the few of his 'gamers' games' that I had yet to play. That was rectified in August (09) and it goes right up there with Ra and Taj Mahal and just behind Tigris & Euphrates (my all-time favourite board game). Like Ra and Taj it has an auction at its heart, but unlike those games there's more to it than just the auction. The auction itself has a nice mechanic and some difficult valuation problems. I also enjoy the bluffing and brinkmanship involved in the sacrifice phase. My only concern is that the power cards might be a little unbalanced and determine the winner in a close game.

RATING: 9.25
One of Kmizia's heavier games (a la Tigris & Euphrates) and once again, the theme is some random ancient civilization. In this case, it works very well because you are building pyramids and the game even plays in two epochs, the old and new kingdom, Egypt here we come ! The game can be very brainburning, especially for first-timers (like I was) because the scoring only takes place in 2 out of 6 rounds and as such, your actions and strategies build up to those phases instead of going for the quick points all the time. I love the auction mechanic that this game uses (the same as in 20th Century). There could be a tad more interaction in later phases, but what the heck, great stuff.

RATING: 7
A tight game about spending just enough money to stay ahead of the other players in building pyramids while saving enough to win bids on choice locations. It's very easy to miss important details since workers, pyramids, and cards all kind of blend together to equal X amount of money and everything ties in to the auction which can end abruptly if you're not careful.

End scores tend to be tight, with the winner usually being the player who barely accomplishes some end game scoring goal. Overall, I don't feel the tension in this one as much as other players, and sometimes the scoring feels artificially close. It's a good Knizia, but by no means my favorite.

RATING: 5.4
This game is fairly straight-forward, but is a fairly dry "convert money into VP" game. The auction mechanic is good in theory, but in practice is rarely that interesting. This is one of the very few Knizia games that feels under-developed: maybe the escalating costs for the auction don't work and stymie back-and-forth action; the power cards don't feel quite right as not drawing a VP card can be a major set-back and so maybe they need to be a separate decck or be draftable; and even the sacrifice round is a little too hard to finesse especially with regards to controlling turn order (which is critical but usually out of your power to determine). The time effects are nicely implemented causing quite different values for the three commodities across the game.

RATING: 7
Likes
The auction mechanic is fun and engaging. There are elements of set collection and area control that are puzzling and fun. I like adding to or stealing from the offering to help yourself. There are lots of ways to block and interact with the other players in this game.

Dislikes
Nothing specific to dislike. Can be a bit of a brain-burner trying to coordinate your sets and the area bonuses and the chaos of the auctions. This is not a bad thing but one is not always in the mood for brain-burning type fun.

RATING: 8
[COLOR=#663300][b][size=11]Amun-Re[/size][/b][/COLOR]
[COLOR=#663300][i]Note: My numeric ratings disregard BGG's associated verbal descriptions.[/i][/COLOR]

[COLOR=#006600]Version played: [/COLOR]2003 Rio Grande Games, English
[COLOR=#006600]Play History: [/COLOR]
 [COLOR=#006600] • 3 players: [/COLOR] None
 [COLOR=#006600] • 4 players: [/COLOR] Twice
 [COLOR=#006600] • 5 players: [/COLOR] None

[COLOR=#006600]Instructions: [/COLOR]Must evaluate.
[COLOR=#006600]Learning Curve: [/COLOR]It's been a while.  It's possible that the learning curve was somewhat steep, but all I recall is that our first play went smoothly.
[COLOR=#006600]Depth: [/COLOR]Definitely strategic, but not at all ponderous. See my comments below.
[COLOR=#006600]Luck: [/COLOR]While the power cards are indeed powerful, they're notably well-balanced.  The difference between which cards you draw is a minuscule matter when compared with the importance of simply having a power card at all.
[COLOR=#006600]Downtime: [/COLOR]As with most middle-weight games, there is potential for downtime, but it wasn't an issue in our games.  In fact, our games had a rather continuous feel of urgency.
[COLOR=#006600]Fiddliness: [/COLOR]Not an issue.
[COLOR=#006600]Components: [/COLOR]The pyramids and building stones are quite nice, and the artwork is both thematic and functional.
[COLOR=#006600]Player Interaction: [/COLOR]High — bidding in particular.
[COLOR=#006600]Conflict: [/COLOR]Nothing direct.
[COLOR=#006600]Theme: [/COLOR]I've heard complaints that the ancient Egypt theme is old hat, but I found that the theme, the rules, and the components all worked well together.

[COLOR=#006600]Travel related: [/COLOR][COLOR=#CC0000]Unsuitable.[/COLOR]
 [COLOR=#006600] • Size: [/COLOR]Large.
 [COLOR=#006600] • Weight: [/COLOR]Heavy.
 [COLOR=#006600] • Table Required: [/COLOR]Yes.
 [COLOR=#006600] • Footprint: [/COLOR]Large.
 [COLOR=#006600] • Component Trackability: [/COLOR]Poor.
 [COLOR=#006600] • Replaceability: [/COLOR]Available as of Jan 2010.

[COLOR=#006600][b]Comments: [/b][/COLOR]

Randomness does not equate with luck.  Amun-Re demonstrates this.  The power cards provide balanced randomness.  You may not know which power cards you'll draw, but most of them will prove useful relatively soon.  It takes us a long time to play, but due to the round/phase structure, there is a sense of urgency throughout. 

Power cards are aptly named.  They're quite powerful.  After playing a second time, I can see that one's long-term strategy must take into account the power cards.  It's great to get bonus cards (a subset of power cards) early, so you can evaluate provinces with long-term payoff in mind. Choosing which provinces to pursue isn't just about immediate rewards.  No matter how enticing a province may appear, if it eliminates a potential bonus, think twice.  (Keep in mind, however,  that all bonus cards are only worth 3 points each, so they can only influence how much you're willing to bid for a province by so much.)

This is not to say that power cards are the key to victory -- only that ignoring them is risky.  It seems to me that it's a good idea to balance one's expenditures between no less than four of the five main avenues (province placement, power cards, farmers, pyramids and sacrifices), but I can imagine that as few as three of the avenues could work, so long as one of the three was power cards — and the [i]correct combination[/i] of power cards at that!

Four of the five sounds like a lot, but this is a rather subtle balancing act, and without power cards to add weight to certain expenditures, each arena must be well-represented. 

[COLOR=#006600][b]Possible variant: [/b][/COLOR]

Scott Nicholson says: "I wish the cards you bought were in two stacks - cards for bonus points and other cards. That would give you some well-needed choice about what type of cards you wanted."

Interesting idea for a variant.

[COLOR=#006600][b]Why we own it: [/b][/COLOR] 

I avoided this game for some time.  I thought we had enough of "that sort of thing".  (Egyptian themed games?  Knizia middle-weights?)  Then we played it.  

Fun.  Challenging.  Engrossing.  

We own it because I enjoy the play throughout.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Interesting game, and enjoyable. Decisions like many of the other great games, such as Puerto Rico and Prince of Florence.

Like many special power card games, you may want to correct slightly to fix problem of losing due to bad card draw. Suggested fix is "draw two, keep one".

In our group, this is a game that (almost) no one dislikes, and no one thinks is the best.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Some of Knizia's older designs may have an evergreen flair, but unfortunately Amun-Re isn't one of those designs. To its credit, it is a touch more thematic than most Knizian classics and incorporates a few really clever mechanisms, such as the collusive sacrificial blind bid and the mid-game board wipe. But the overall package is peppered with tiny (yet important) rules exceptions and feels a little too dry. 

Another part of the problem is that elements of Amun-Re have since been adapted into more interesting titles which I'd rather play. The displacement-type auction is put to good use in designs like [thing=26566][/thing], and [thing=28720][/thing] implements the mid-game board wipe very well. The final nail in Amun-Re's sarcophagus is that the power cards are a ridiculous gamble. Given that cash is constantly tight, the idea of paying for a blind draw which could be worthless is terribly unappealing. A functional yet dated design.

RATING: 8
Tentative rating.

Another Kniza auction machine, albeit with a slightly better sense of theme. Players are bidding on parcels of land in Egypt. The they use money to build Pyramids, place famers, etc.

There isn't the usual Kniza scoring twist but players score once midgame and re-bid on all provinces again and score again at the end. This means that if you spend too much money building early on and fail to reacquire the land, you're helping your opponent!

Heard that HiG asked Kniza to develop the power cards: good choice as it helps deepen gameplay.

Runs smoothly although I think that it's a little fiddly with all the components to move around the board.

RATING: 9
Yet another refinement of a Knizia auction game that everyone liked.  The board is (approximately) a 4x4 grid of provinces with the Nile running down the middle.  An auction system ensures everyone buys control of one province a round.  You then stock up your provinces by buying farmers, who make money, and pyramids, which make victory points (in a variety of obtuse ways).  Everyone then &quot;sacrifices&quot; additional money into the harvest: the combined sum of everyone&#039;s sacrifice determines how much money each farmer gets you.  After explaining all this, you&#039;d better play sample rounds, as there are all sorts of rules that only make sense after trying it.  You can spend money on random power cards, which either give you little perks or reward you victory points for shaping your strategy in unusual ways.  After everyone has populated 3 provinces, you calculate score.  You then clear out everything BUT the pyramids, do another 3 rounds, then do a final scoring.  To win you must combine your provinces and power cards into a strategy that finds as many different ways to score victory points without costly head to head clashes with others.  Monetary decisions include competitive bidding for provinces, working around penalties for buying too much of the same thing, and determining if harvests benefit you or your competitors more.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3
This is undeniably a good game.  I can see how people could love it, I guess.  I just have a hard time getting into it. Perhaps, because it is dry and mechanical. Perhaps, becuase it takes everything you have away from you as an easy out to prevent a runaway leader. 

I think that the easiest thing to forget about the game is that it is purely a "group think" system. Yes, there is a heavy level of clinical analysis (if you  want that sort of thing in a game), but it is irritatingly difficult to gauge what anyone values a region at.  Being outbid is eyerolling as it becomes incrementaly more difficult to remain in play. It seems you want to start with what the group value is for an item, but even still you are subjected to the folly and whimsey of another players disinterest in changing their strategy forcing themselves to perhaps pay too high, but leaving you with crap for options.  

Clearly that is the game. Usually a game that forces changes in strategies are exciting to me, but I feel that your options are dictated arbitrarily by others that, while justifiably, sometimes it comes from the betterment of their game, I feel that often time it comes from tertiary moves when there are no other engaging options available.  It also seems that there is often a dramatic difference in pricing for regions, but because different regions yield asymetrical results often a player can continually walk away with the regions they want for nothing, because other players aren't pursuing their strategy, yet there is no real incentive to keep that player honest as their regions would only set you out of the game if you were to win it.

The components are quite nice, and there is a fine balance to the game.  But at the end of the day, what it all comes down to for me was that there was just no fun factor.  I would probably play it again if someone offered, but I certainly wouldn't ask for it to come off of the shelf.  One of those classic games that walks a fine line between irritating and boring.

UPDATE: This has recently been downgraded to a 3.  After playing an utterly unexciting and repetitious game of Amun-Re I was made aware of just how much this game isn't for me.  BTW here is some advice, don't draw all Master Builder cards.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
3 rounds then score; then repeat. Each round, auction (using an innovative method of predefined bid increments; if you're overbid you must bid on something ELSE -- similar to Vegas Showdown) for a province. Each province has different characteristics (# farmers allowed, earnings modifiers, # power card purchases allowed, bonuses awarded to auction winner). After the auction, players take turns buying stuff -- again with escalating costs (first is 1, second is 2, ...): power cards, then farmers, then building stones (for pyramids). The round ends with everyone making a blind sacrifice (or stealing 3 gold); non-stealers get a reward(s). Finally, scoring: get points for pyramids, sets of pyramids, most pyramids on one (or both) sides of the Nile, control of 3 particular provinces (VP payoff related to total sacrifice amount), and various power card bonuses. (Power cards also modify play throughout the game). After the first scoring, the process is repeated using the SAME provinces that came up previously (but with farmers removed; only pyramids and building stones stay from early kingdom (1st phase) to late kingdom. Most points at end wins!

This is a fun game; auctions can be tough, and the sacrifice for Ra is fun - especially since the rewards themselves can change scoring. The auction mechanism works well, too. We don't get this on the table much, but I've played a bunch on spielbyweb now - excellent implementation!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a standard auction game with one pretty unique feature that really elevates it and makes you think differently about the game than most others.

In this players will bid to win auctions of regions at the start of each round.  The regions will provide many different benefits from income, to farmers that will increase income based on that round's harvest, to building blocks for building pyramids, to special action cards that give players additional abilities.  After each player has won an area they spend money to buy stuff.  Either farmers, action cards, or building blocks.  Everything except the building blocks is limited by the region(s) they own.  The building blocks are arguably the most important from a scoring perspective though as once you buy three for an area it becomes a pyramid and those are worth the most points.

The really unique feature for this game though is that after 3 rounds the board gets scrubbed...almost.  Everything disappears, including players ownership of regions, except the pyramids which stand the test of time.  So for the second half of the game when regions show up at auction you may be bidding on an area where there are already some pyramids built and like I said those are worth the most points.

This creates a really interesting dynamic.  Every pyramid you build in the first half of the game could go to another player or not even show up at auction at all during the second half.

Not to mention the rest of the game is decent too, it doesn't do a whole lot new, at least not for 2015 (this game was originally released 12 years ago), but is a more standard auction/economic game.  It's this neat changing of ages that really makes it stand out.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[RGG 2003 Edition] {Egypt; blind-bidding, bidding, secret-goal, area-majority, epic, utility cards; optimal 5p} I have been to Egypt before and have an enjoyable time. For this reason, I like Egypt-themed games. And, I bought Amun-Re without much thinking. It turns out that Amun-Re is a decent strategy game with high replayability but a thin theme. Knizia nicely integrates several mechanics into Amun-Re: open bidding with multiple objects, blind bidding, area-controls and resources management. Despite the multiple mechanics, it is not overly complicated. Yet, it takes some time to explain the rules clearly.

Player interaction is strong in Amun-Re: there is strong competition in bidding the provinces, building pyramids and contribution to Amun-Re.  Players have to concentrate on how each player acts. Without knowing what others will do, a player can hardly win this game.

Amun-Re is multi-faceted - there are multiple ways of winning.  In addition, it is a tight resources management game.  As a result, it can be agonizing. Yet it is only moderately deep and down time is not very strong.  It is a game requiring good planning and is quite unforgiving. Since resources are tight and actions are limited, a player needs a definite plan to collect victory points: which provinces to occupy, how much contribution to make, etc. Bidding part is also tricky - the EV of each province changes during each turn of the game. It's also hard to determine the contribution to Amun-Re. 

Knizia makes Amun-Re even more dynamic with the 2-epoch system. If construction buildings are not destroyed in an epoch, the game will be static and everyone can calculate the score determinedly. With the 2-epoch system, it thwarts this deep calculation because everything except pyramids will be destroyed after the first epoch.

RATING: 6
I was amased  to see this rated Knizia offering suffering from so many problems.

The game provides the perfect environment for:

1) Analysis Paralysis - Our first five player game went on for over 3 hours.

2) End game Bonus Cards that offer an 'all or nothing' bonus and no incremental rewards, for just missing out.

3) This is incredibly compounded by one of the  worst 'Kingmaking' mechanics I have ever witnessed. A trailing player with little or nothing to play for but with enough funds, can choose which of the two leaders he wants to screw with. As the leaders will probably have been forced to invest everything, in an 'all or nothing' region, the losing player chooses the winner abitrarily, presumably based on who he thinks may be leading at the time or some other such personal reasoning. Quite absurd.

Sure, there are some things in hindsight that I could have done to mitigate this, like trying to conceal any interest in my current Region but this would be covering for a much broken and easily abused mechanic and I no longer feel inclined to try.  

Amun Re is an absolutely awful experience in the wrong circumstances and fully deserving of one of the lowest ratings that I have ever given to a game. 

A BGG rating of 2 is by definition a game I would never play again.

It has some interesting mechanics like the bidding mechanism. This works brilliantly in games like Evo where it allows you to improve you dinosaurs' ability by small increments. But Knizia is mis-using it here as an integral part of the game for major game altering effects. 

I choose to outbid all the other reviewers that made this a top 50 'hit' and relegate it to the bottom of the table. Why? Because I can and have nothing to lose by doing so. 

Following in the footsteps of Ra: with it's a fast, tense and well integrated mechanisms, Amun Re demonstrates that even the better game designers can miss the mark by a country mile.

Advice: Stay well clear!

RATING: 8
Nearly all games have a theme but there seem to be few games where the mechanics of the game actually have any relation to the theme.  This is one of those games, and for that, I think it should be commended.  It is a fun and interesting game that uses a number of different concepts during each turn.  There is an auction phase, an purchase and asset management phase and a rather fun secret offering phase.  While player interaction on the board is minimal there is plenty of fun interaction during the bidding and offering phases and there is plenty of opportunity to hamper your opponents' progress.  My only criticism is that, even though it is not a difficult game to play, the rules are not particularly well written for a game that has quite a few different phases that need explaining.  But, that small negative is outweighed by the positives, fun interaction, great looking game, varied mechanics & strategies and a game that actually seems to match its theme.

RATING: 9
Amun-Re is right up there with the likes of El Grand, Princes of Florence and Puerto Rico.  It is awash with theme, and looks absolutely stunning. The components are top notch, with the pyramids and stones having an authentic tactile feel to them. The artwork is gorgeous, and as with all Hans Im Gluck games, the meaning of the cards can be understood straight away.  

This is an easy game to play. Its not a heavy brain burner, but the different phases during each round offers variety in game-play and strategy and gives the players plenty to think about. It plays quickly, and the advertised play time of 90 minutes is spot on.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.2
This game requires no further introduction, I am sure. Each player takes on the role of Pharaoh aiming to build the most pyramids and bring the greatest glory to Amun Re and his fellow Gods.

It incorporates auctions, farming, the clever use of power cards, offering to the Gods and a lot more. The theme is there, even if this is often held against the honourable Mr. Knizia, and you feel present, you feel like the Pharaoh trying to best your annoying family members all trying to supplant you.

The game plays from 3-5 players and best at 5, according to the BGG community. Our one play with 3 players showed the potential in this title, but also that it requires accomplished players to function. If one player is unaware of the intricacies of the game and its mechanics, the others may well feel the sting.

It requires an investment of time and through it a familiarity with the game to play smoothly and in a decent time frame. Our play took up to 3 hours, which did not feel long, but repeat plays at this length would - there is not enough to hold the interest as such.

Our caveat is, however, based on the required player count. The game was purchased on its reputation, without regard to the nature of our gaming group. It has for this reason been sold off.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The more I play this, the more I like it.  Great economic game system.  Does involve quite a bit of calculation to  figure your optimal bid and build, but I like some of that in a game.  Two mechanics that stand out are the clever auction process (bids in fixed increments that make overbidding progressively more expensive) and the fact that pyramids stay on the board between ages but ownership of provinces is re-auctioned, which means your buildings in the old kingdom can benefit someone else in the new if they have more gold with which to buy the built-up provinces.

Only real downside to this game is that it is so much better with 5 than with fewer players that it isn't worth playing unless there are 5 at the table.

Rating by number of players:
3 players: 5
4 players: 7
5 players: 9

RATING: 10
Fun game.  Many decisions, a lot to look at, but at the same not an overwhelming amount to look at.  A gamer's game for nice medium weight.  I like how my brain is forced to work in this game.  High replayability.  Actually, the more I play this game the more I like it.  The nuances and tricks of this game slowly reveal themselves.  I think thats incredible.  Amun-Re is 70% resource management and 30% group-think.  The group-think aspect of the game is fantastic.  I like games that shine when every opponenet is comfortable with the game system and competent.  Amun-Re is one.  For me the game scales from 5-5 players.  I'm not sure how well it plays with 3 or 4, because I never play it with that number.


The sacrifice is one of my favorite game mechanics.  This is why this one is a keeper.  The more I play this the more I like it.

This is one of Dr. Knizia's most thematic and engaging games, plus the pieces/board are really nicely done.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Wow, what a great game [thing=5404][/thing] is!

Every decision has some sort of consequence: do I try to take this awesome province, but it will cost me. Then will I have money for stones, cards and farmers? And how much will I have left to give to Amun-Re? If Amun-Re stays in the 1 or 2 spot, my farmers won't help me enough for my next round. And this goes on and on. I really enjoy this sort of mind exercise. There is room for AP, but this is a problem of the players, not the game.

That happens because:
- At first you will need money for provinces (might not be much, will depend on how much the others want to fight for a place) - do you go for the one with a free stone? With place for farmers? With garanteed income? The one near the water? The one in the West bank?;
- Now you will need money for stones, farmers and cards - stones build pyramids, that translates to points, but you must remember that, in the New Kingdom (the second stage of the game), other player might take hold of the province;
- Now you will need money to offer to Amun-Re - how much will you get if it goes to 3? How much will the others? How bad do you want those three items? Maybe two will be enough, but how much will get you second place? It is time to go with the -3 card, get nothing more than 3 money, but you just might hinder the others deeply;
- Now you will receive some money, but the amount will depend on the level of the sacrifices for Amun-Re, and also due to the income provided by some provinces. You should have give more to Amun-Re? Well, now it is to late.

[thing=5404][/thing] might just be the game I was looking for to close the Holy Trinity of Knizia's games, along side [thing=3][/thing] and [thing=12][/thing]. And yes, they all can stay in the same collection, because they play so different from each other - [thing=12][/thing] is closer in theme and because both are auction games, but [thing=5404][/thing] didn't feel to me as much as an auction game, more like a work placement where the workers are money, piramids, farmers. It is hard to put into words, but if you play [thing=5404][/thing] and [thing=12][/thing] back to back, you will know what I mean.

The game also has nice bits, and the artwork, though not great, does its job rather well - the action cards are easy to understand even for players learning it.

I rate [thing=5404][/thing] "only" at 8, mainly because I need to explore it further.

RATING: 6.5
Not bad for my first play with 5 players.  Finished third 2 points out of first place without ever pulling a scoring power card.  Part of this was card draws and the other part was that 3 of the players were experienced and kept hoarding the scoring cards whenever they picked them up.  With any luck, I could have been a first time winner.   

I see the potential for king making in this game by the player in last place.  To be perfectly honest, I believed I was way out of the game (forgot about the temple scoring - I had 2 temples) and decided to screw the player in first place by competing with him for the 5 point bonus for most pyramids.  Turns out I really helped myself by gaining the 5 points and he still finished 1st 2 points ahead.

All in all a decent game that will move much faster now that I know what the cards do and don't have to keep looking them up.  Also, a power card hand limit would definitely balance things out. 

My rating may go up depending on how my second game plays out.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Taught by BeyondMonopoly and others at PrezCon 04.  After two plays, this is a great, tight game.  There are alot of choices and many paths to victory, but not too surprisingly for a Knizia game, balance and efficiency are key.  You can pay through the nose to get exactly what you want, but you will be beaten by a frugal player.  You can be frugal and get by, but the player willing to pay for valuable things will win.  The correct path is probably in the middle and influenced by the people you are playing with.

Update:  Played my 3rd game and came in 2nd for the third time...  This time was with my regular game group.  The game played out a little differently, but still ended up with three players within a three point span at the lead.  Raised my rating from an 8 to a 9.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
I liked it a lot less after a 2nd play (rating from 8 to 6.5). The decisions in each phase are tricky and tension-inducing, which I like a lot. The auction, when taken alone, is great and leads to all kinds of double think and wonderfuly "gamey" feelings. The sacrifice too, a nice bit of fun-- even though I am not a huge fan of blind bidding (except in the case of Raj!) this is ok. And how to spend the money? Nail-biter for sure, especially when money is tight. Where it doesn't work for me is how all three things come together and interact once the very random cards are thrown in the mix. I think the variation in powers that the cards give leads to too much luck. In the two games I've played, I cannot look at various moments and say, "ahh that is when I  made a mistake!" instead I say to myself, "Ahhh, that is when I got so #$%$ (un)lucky." The victory seems to come down to whoever has the most "@#$#% lucky!" moments. When I presented this opinion to my game friends they suggested I was missing how much planning led to getting lucky-- or the "You make your own luck" defense of luck. I'm happy to play another few times to hopefully learn they are right! I'll revise accordingly then. But in the meantime, it gets added to the list of Knizia games that my freinds love, but leave me sort of meh. (ps. I'm *not* looking at you Modern Art!)

RATING: 6
It's an auction game blended with an area control game, and it comes together into a nice mesh of both.  There are several different sorts of auctions in the game, but it never feels strictly like an auction game; instead, it feels unique and interesting.  There are some issues with the randomness of the power cards granting a player a large number of points at the right time, but you also have to plan for some of those situations.  Best with 5 players, I think.  Its main issue with me is that it just feels very much like a standard Euro cube-pusher, and it doesn't give the game much staying power, even though it's a decent overall game.

RATING: 9
My first plays were all with five players, so I was shocked to discover how differently this game plays with three -- and not simply because certain provinces never come into play. The value of the provinces varies greatly depending on when they come out and how many players you have, and that aspect of the game really appeals to me.

You have to judge what others will do on their turn, which will affect the sacrifice, your income (and theirs), and your bonus (if any), which drives the later rounds. Everything is interconnected and driven by the actions of the players, so reading your opponents is crucial to good play. This makes the game tougher for newbies, but hopefully they'll stick with it, perhaps on Spielbyweb where they can play many games quickly.

RATING: N/A
Auction strategy to place farmers for income and build pyramids for area control of Eygypt.  One of my favorite auction mechanics placing a player pawn on escalating value card tracks until all players have won a territory ( works best with full player counts).  I love the themactic relationship of the destructive but revitalizing powers of the Nile river floods washing away the farmlands along the delta and how this plays on short and long term strategy of the re-auctions.  I also like the elegant market mechanic for increasing unit costs with larger quantity purchases.  Visually, the appearance of pyramids over the rounds is satisfying and I love how it conveys the passage of time. The gifts to the Gods phase is another highlight that drives opposing player objectives for points or income mathing out the gift value of your territories while predicting the gifts of the other players (also better with higher play counts). Lowest common denominator scoring is another strategy factor that I enjoy.

RATING: 8
Before I started playing a more diverse set of games (wargames, silly games,  Ameritrash games, sex games - OK, maybe not the last one), Amun Re was easily in my top 10.

It's a game of remarkable restraint, in that a player who establishes a significant lead in the first half - which is often the result of fiscal irresponsibility - isn't guaranteed to maintain that lead and win in the second half.

I'm always a sucker for unique auction mechanics, too, and the must-bid-elsewhere-if-overbid mechanic was original when Amun Re was first released.  To add to the game's depth, the changing province values (based on pyramids not being washed away, etc.) and bonus scoring cards are bound to shake up players' "how much is this really worth?" bid appraisals.

The only points of complaint are that the randomly drawn bonus cards can swing games, and the game just doesn't scale well.  Playing with five is the only option for a truly competitive game.

RATING: 9
Possibly my favourite Reiner Knizia game, and since I have more by him than any other designer, that's saying something. (Although that's partly because he's so prolific.)

The territories use a very interesting and different auction mechanic, and the sacrifice uses blind bidding with the twist that the total matters as well as the individual bids, and some players will want to lower that total rather than raise it.

Having only played it twice, I don't have any in-depth comments yet, but I loved it both times and hope to get playing it again soon!

RATING: 7.5
Really liked my first play, but we missed several rules that could have had a significant impact. Lots of nice options, but like Merchants of Amsterdam, I got a very strong feeling that the game helps the rich get richer -- if you have more money, you get to spend more on your actions to get what you need to generate more money. Fall behind that curve, either through poor decision making, poor luck of the draw from the Power Card deck or a poor farmer payout when you really need it, and it seems like you can quickly find yourself out of the game by the halfway point. I've enjoyed my subsequent plays more, but I'm not completely convinced of the game's long-term strategic replay value, despite really liking the components, mechanics and decisions (few though they seem to be).

RATING: N/A
Sobre una carta los jugadores subastan por una provincia. En ella colocan piedras para construir pirámides y contratan trabajadores para sembrar. Las cartas dan poderes y permiten puntuar de acuerdo a diferentes requisitos.
